011                                                A BSTRACT
012
013            Federated Bilevel Optimization (FBO) enables training machine learning models
014            with nested structures across distributed devices while preserving data privacy.
015            However, current FBO methods often impose restrictive assumptions, particularly
016
               the requirement of strong convexity in the lower-level objective. To overcome this
               limitation, we propose a first-order stochastic gradient method for general FBO
017
               problems, leveraging a Moreau envelope-based min-max optimization reformu-
018
               lation to handle potentially non-convex lower-level objectives. Unlike implicit
019            gradient methods, our approach eliminates the need for second-order derivative
020            information. We also establish rigorous theoretical guarantees for convergence
021            rate and communication complexity, demonstrating linear speedup as the number
022            of devices increases. Numerical experiments validate the effectiveness and effi-
023            ciency of our method, showing comparable or superior performances in challenging
024            scenarios, including federated loss function tuning on imbalanced datasets and
025            federated hyper-representation.
026
027
028   1    I NTRODUCTION
029
030   Bilevel optimization has gained prominence in machine learning due to its effectiveness in solving
031
      nested structural problems, with applications in areas such as hyperparameter tuning (Franceschi
      et al., 2018; Bao et al., 2021; Sinha et al., 2024), meta-learning (Franceschi et al., 2018; Jia & Zhang,
032
      2024), and reinforcement learning (Hu et al., 2024b; Yang et al., 2024). These approaches rely on
033
      access to the entire dataset, raising concerns about privacy leakage. With the growing importance of
034   data privacy, federated bilevel optimization (FBO) has emerged as a crucial paradigm. FBO enables
035   collaborative learning on distributed datasets while preserving individual privacy, addressing complex
036   nested optimization problems such as federated reinforcement learning (Ruan et al., 2024; Yin et al.,
037   2024).
038
      FBO combines the challenges of bilevel optimization and federated learning, addressing both nested
039
      optimization difficulties and the complexities of distributed learning. Existing algorithms, including
040   AID-based methods such as Huang et al. (2023); Huang (2022), and ITD-based methods such as
041   Xiao & Ji (2023), often require strong convexity in the lower-level objective functions to compute
042   federated hypergradients via the implicit function theorem (Kearns, 1989). The recently proposed
043   single-loop SimFBO algorithm (Yang et al., 2023), based on SOBA (Dagr√©ou et al., 2022), also
044   depends on strong convexity at the lower level. These requirements significantly limit the applicability
045   of current algorithms, as many problems naturally involve non-convex lower-level objectives, such as
046   in Federated Transfer Learning (Liang et al., 2022; Zhang & Li, 2021).
047   There have been numerous recent studies addressing the issue of non-strong-convexity in the lower-
048   level of bilevel optimization (BLO) under the single-machine (non-federated) setting (Liu et al., 2022;
049   Kwon et al., 2023; Huang, 2023b; Yao et al., 2023; Liu et al., 2024; Kwon et al., 2024). Given these
050   significant advancements in addressing lower-level non-strong-convexity in BLO, a natural question
051   arises:
052
053        Can we develop a stochastic gradient method for FBO that does not require strong convexity
           in the lower-level problem, while reducing computation and communication costs?


                                                         1
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


054
      To achieve this goal, several challenging issues must be addressed.
055
056   Inapplicability of the implicit function theorem. Current approaches to FBO rely heavily on
057   the implicit function theorem, particularly for computing hypergradients‚ÄĒa key component of the
058
      optimization process. While powerful, the implicit function theorem imposes strict conditions on the
      problem structure, most notably the requirement for strong convexity in the lower-level objective.
059
060   High computational cost of Hessian-Jacobian computations. Existing FBO algorithms often
061   require matrix-vector products involving the Hessian or Jacobian matrices of the lower-level objective,
062   which are computationally expensive. Developing new algorithms that eliminate the need for Hessian
063   or Jacobian computations is essential for scaling to large-scale applications.
064   Linear speedup challenges under lower-level non-strong convexity. In federated settings, achieving
065   linear speedup with respect to the number of devices is critical for fully leveraging distributed
066   resources. However, it remains unclear whether convergence rates can attain linear speedup in the
067   absence of strong convexity at the lower level in FBO.
068
069   1.1   M AIN CONTRIBUTIONS
070
071   To address the aforementioned challenges, inspired by the recent advances of Liu et al. (2024) in
072   bilevel optimization, we develop a first-order stochastic gradient method, named MeFBO, for feder-
073   ated bilevel optimization based on a Moreau envelope-based minimax optimization reformulation,
074   which allows for nonconvexity in the lower-level problem.
075
             ‚ÄĘ To the best of our knowledge, MeFBO is the first federated bilevel optimization approach
076
               that uses only first-order derivatives while addressing nonconvex lower-level problems.
077
078          ‚ÄĘ We establish rigorous theoretical guarantees for convergence rate and sample complexity,
079            demonstrating linear speedup with respect to the number of participating clients (sampling
080            without replacement).
081          ‚ÄĘ We validate MeFBO empirically on three federated bilevel learning tasks: federated hyper-
082            representation, federated loss function tuning on imbalanced datasets, and federated data
083            hyper-cleaning. The results demonstrate that MeFBO achieves comparable or superior, and
084            more robust, performance compared to existing FBO approaches.
085
086
      Table 1: Comparison of MeFBO with closely related FBO approaches. Below, LL-convexity refers
087   to the convexity condition of the lower-level objective function. LL-Lipschitz continuity denotes
088   the Lipschitz continuity requirement of the lower-level problem, which involves multiple orders of
089   information. H-J free indicates that the method does not require Hessian or Jacobian computations.
090   For simplicity, we exclude methods with momentum-based acceleration or those designed to handle
091   system-level heterogeneity.

                   Method                   LL-convexity       continuity

103
104
      As this work leverages insights from both Liu et al. (2024) and (Yang et al., 2023), we explain the
      differences from the most relevant literature.
105
106   Compared to our work, MEHA by Liu et al. (2024) is limited to deterministic settings for non-
107   federated bilevel optimization. In contrast, even when reduced to the non-federated setting, our
      MeFBO is a stochastic algorithm with convergence rate and sample complexity guarantees.


                                                        2
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


108
      More importantly, efficiently solving federated bilevel optimization problems is even more challenging
109   due to the need to preserve privacy and reduce communication costs, which requires a variable number
110   of local updates on the client side. This adds complexity to both algorithm design and theoretical
111   analysis compared to non-federated bilevel optimization. For example, local client updates introduce
112   challenges in setting algorithm hyperparameters (such as client and server learning rates) and in
113   analyzing communication and sample complexity.
114
      As a result, compared to the analysis in Liu et al. (2024), in the federated learning setting, we must
115   control the bounds of client drifts and harmonize them with the variance from local stochastic gradient
116   estimation and errors from client sampling to achieve convergence. Specifically, it is crucial that these
117   bounds (to be established) explicitly depend on the effects of local update rounds and the number
118   of participating clients per communication round, as discussed in Section 3.2. This significantly
119   increases the complexity of the theoretical analysis.
120
      The comparison between SimFBO (Yang et al., 2023) and our work is highlighted in Table 1. The
121   key difference lies in the starting points of algorithm design. SimFBO is designed and analyzed
122   from the perspective of hypergradient estimation, relying on the strong convexity assumption and
123   involving second-order derivatives. In contrast, we leverage insights from Liu et al. (2024) and use
124   local stochastic gradient estimators.
125
126   1.2   R ELATED WORK
127
128   We provide a concise review of recent works directly related to ours, with a more comprehensive
129   review presented in Section G.
130   FedNest (Tarzanagh et al., 2022), one of the earliest FBO methods, is a federated alternating
131   stochastic gradient method (FedNest) that uses AID-based hypergradient estimation to address
132   general federated nested problems. Xiao & Ji (2023) introduced an FBO algorithm employing
133   ITD-based hypergradient estimation, though these approaches do not achieve linear speedup. The
134   study by Huang et al. (2023), which uses AID-based hypergradient estimation, achieves linear
135   speedup without requiring full client participation in every communication round. Recent FBO
136
      methods, such as SimFBO (Yang et al., 2023) and FedBiOAcc (Li et al., 2023), draw inspiration
      from SOBA (Dagr√©ou et al., 2022). These approaches transform a linear system problem into
137
      a quadratic one, improving computational efficiency within a single-loop algorithmic framework.
138
      Notably, FedBiOAcc incorporates a momentum-based technique. While these algorithms have made
139   significant progress, they continue to rely on Hessian matrix computations and are constrained by the
140   requirement for lower-level strong convexity (LLSC).
141
142
      In addition to the aforementioned works, there is a growing body of research on bilevel optimization
      in asynchronous settings, such as those by Jiao et al. (2022) and Li et al. (2024). Furthermore, FBO
143
      has demonstrated promising practical applications, particularly in the fine-tuning of large language
144
      models (LLMs) within federated settings. For instance, Wu et al. (2024) investigates the use of
145   FBO for local fine-tuning of LLMs. As noted in Table 2 of Wu et al. (2024), these models can be
146   optimized using either single-level or bilevel approaches. Notably, the bilevel optimization method,
147   FedBiOT, proposed by Wu et al. (2024), exhibits significant advantages over single-level optimization,
148   especially in scenarios involving hierarchical problem structures.
149
150

      Remark and other possible algorithmic designs. (1) Since MeFBO uses only stochastic gradient
263
      estimators, it is a first-order stochastic gradient method, making it significantly different from existing
264   FBO methods. Another notable feature of MeFBO is its ability to handle constraints on both the upper-
265   and lower-level variables. Additionally, the corresponding projection operators are implemented
266   exclusively on the server side, enhancing MeFBO‚Äôs practicality and efficiency. (2) MeFBO also
267   offers strong extensibility. For instance, instead of using SGDA as in (9), one could employ other
268   stochastic gradient estimation techniques (e.g., SAGA, STORM) to develop more advanced federated
269   stochastic bilevel optimization algorithms. Refer to recent advances in bilevel optimization Dagr√©ou
      et al. (2022); Chen et al. (2023); Huang (2023b); Chu et al. (2024).

      3.1     A SSUMPTIONS
273
274   We make the following assumptions in the theoretical investigation.
275
      Assumption 3.1. For the upper-level objective, the following conditions hold:
276
277       (i) The UL objective F is bounded below, i.e., F :=       inf     F(x, y) > ‚ąí‚ąě.
278                                                             (x,y)‚ąąX√óY

279
       (ii) For each i ‚ąą [n], fi (x, y) is twice continuously differentiable, and Lf -Lipschitz continuous.
280
            The gradients ‚ąáfi (x, y) is L1 -Lipschitz continuous, i.e., fi (x, y) is L1 -smooth.
281
282   (iii) For each i ‚ąą [n], ‚ąáfi (x, y;                                 ‚ąáfi (x, y). Furthermore, there exists
                                        őĺi ) is an unbiased estimator of
283         a constant ŌÉf such that E ‚ą•‚ąáfi (x, y) ‚ąí ‚ąáfi (x, y; őĺi )‚ą•2 ‚Č§ ŌÉf2 .
284
      Assumption 3.2. For the lower-level objective, the following conditions hold:
285
286       (i) For each i ‚ąą [n], gi (x, y) is twice continuously differentiable, and gi (¬∑, y) is Lg -Lipschitz
287           continuous for any y, and gi (x, y) is L2 -smooth.
288
289    (ii) For each i ‚ąą [n],
                             ‚ąági (x, y; ő∂i ) is an unbiased estimator
                                                                      of ‚ąági (x, y), and there exists a constant
290         ŌÉg such that E ‚ą•‚ąági (x, y) ‚ąí ‚ąági (x, y; ő∂i )‚ą•2 ‚Č§ ŌÉg2 .
291                                               Pn                                     2
      (iii) There exists a constant ‚ąÜ such that     i=1 wi ‚ą•‚ąáy gi (x, y) ‚ąí ‚ąáy G(x, y)‚ą•       ‚Č§ ‚ąÜ2 .
292
293
      Assumption 3.1(ii) and Assumption 3.2(i) impose smoothness and Lipschitz continuity conditions.
294   Notably, we only require the Lipschitz continuity of the first-order derivatives, a key distinction
295   from other FBO literature, which typically also assumes the Lipschitz continuity of second-order
296   derivatives. Assumption 3.1(iii) and Assumption 3.2(ii) are standard assumptions for unbiased
297   gradient estimators and variance bounds in stochastic gradients. In federated learning, Assumption
298   3.2(iii) is commonly used to bound data heterogeneity. The heterogeneity parameter ‚ąÜ represents the
299   level of data heterogeneity, with ‚ąÜ = 0 corresponds to the homogeneous data setting.
300   Remark 3.3. (1) Assumption 3.2(iii) is used to mitigate the impact of client drifts in y (t) and őł(t) on
301   the final convergence. It employs a single parameter ‚ąÜ to describe the degree of heterogeneity, as
302   also used in Li et al. (2023, Assumption 3.5). This differs from Yang et al. (2023, Assumption 4),
303   which uses two parameters. (2) The upper-level objective does not require a similar assumption to
304   Assumption 3.2(iii) because fi (x, y) is assumed to be Lipschitz continuous with respect to both x
305
      and y in Assumption 3.1(ii).
306
307   3.2     C ONVERGENCE RESULT AND COMPLEXITY ANALYSIS
308
      In this section, we provide the convergence rate and sample complexity of MeFBO under Assumptions
309
      3.1 and 3.2. For simplicity, we let P := |C (t) | for all t. When the lower-level problem of bilevel
310
      optimization is strongly convex and unconstrained, the hypergradient norm is typically used as a
311
      stationary measure for algorithms. Unfortunately, this measure is not easily extendable to nonconvex
312   lower-level objectives. Therefore, we introduce local surrogates.
313
314   Inspired by Liu et al. (2024), and by leveraging the stationarity condition of problem (4), we introduce
315
      the following stationarity measure for nonconvex federated bilevel optimization:
316                                                                                 2
                               Rt (x, y) := [dist (0, ‚ąáő•ct (x, y) + NX√óY (x, y))] .                        (13)
317
318   where NX√óY (x, y) is the normal cone of X √ó Y at (x, y). Clearly, Rt (x, y) is well-defined when
319   the hypergradient is. We refer readers to Lemma A.13 in Liu et al. (2024) for a comparison of these
320   two criteria. Furthermore, Rt (x, y) = 0 if and only if 0 ‚ąą ‚ąáő•ct (x, y) + NX√óY (x, y), i.e., the point
321
      (x, y) is a stationary point of problem (4).
322   Theorem 3.4 (Fixed step size). Fix the number of communication rounds T , local update rounds ŌĄ ,
323   and the number P of participating clients per communication round. Assume that Assumptions 3.1
      and 3.2 hold. Let ct = c(t + 1)p with c > 0, p ‚ąą (0, 1/4), and ő≥ ‚ąą (0, 2L1 2 ). Consider fixed server

      Note that the server step sizes in Theorem 3.4 are constant with respect to the number P of partic-
334
      ipating clients, but the client step sizes scale with P ‚ąí1/2 . The proof of Theorem 3.4 refers to that
335
      of Theorem D.11 in Appendix D.4. A proof sketch is provided in Appendix D.2. Additionally, we
336   present theoretical results for the decreasing step size in Appendix C.
337
338   Several remarks about Theorem 3.4 are as follows: (1) By Theorem 3.4, MeFBO achieves a linear
339
      convergence speedup with respect to the number of participating clients (sampling without replace-
      ment); (2) When n = 1 , MeFBO reduces to a first-order stochastic gradient algorithm for nonconvex
340
      bilevel optimization with a convergence rate of O(T ‚ąí1/2 ) by setting ŌĄ = 1 in Theorem 3.4; (3) In
341
      the case of full client participation (i.e., P = n), Theorem 3.4 suggests that increasing the number ŌĄ
342
      of local update steps can help improve the convergence rate. In contrast, for partial client participa-
343   tion, increasing ŌĄ may negatively affect the convergence rate. Theorem 3.4 highlights an important
344   trade-off in the selection of local update rounds ŌĄ . Next, we further analyze the communication and
345   sample complexity of MeFBO.
346   Corollary 3.5 (Fixed step size). Under the setting of Theorem 3.4, we have the following results:
347
348       (i) In the case of full client participation, setting ŌĄ = O(T ), the per-client sample complexity is
349           ŌĄ T = O(ŌĶ‚ąí2 ) and the communication complexity T = O(ŌĶ‚ąí1 ).
350
       (ii) For partial client participation, setting ŌĄ = O(1), the per-client sample complexity is ŌĄ T =
351
            O(P ‚ąí2 ŌĶ‚ąí2 ) and the communication complexity T = O(P ‚ąí2 ŌĶ‚ąí2 ).
352
353   For partial client participation, we set ŌĄ = O(1) because further increasing ŌĄ does not improve
354   the final convergence rate due to the dominant estimator O(P ‚ąí1 ŌĄ 1/2 T ‚ąí1/2 ) in (14). The proof of
355   Corollary 3.5 is provided in Appendix D.5.
356
357
      4     N UMERICAL E XPERIMENTS
358
359
      We evaluate MeFBO on three federated bilevel learning tasks: federated hyper-representation,
360   federated loss function tuning on imbalanced datasets, and federated data hyper-cleaning. We
361   compare its performance with other FBO baselines, including SimFBO (Yang et al. (2023)), and
362   FedNest (Tarzanagh et al. (2022)) and LFedNest (Tarzanagh et al. (2022)). Although the motivation
363   and theoretical analyses of these algorithms (except for MeFBO) rely on the strong convexity of
364   the lower-level problem, all of them can be implemented in nonconvex scenarios. Following the
365   experimental setup in Tarzanagh et al. (2022); Li et al. (2023), we use the MNIST dataset (LeCun
366   et al. (1998)) with i.i.d., non-i.i.d., and imbalanced data partitioning methods. To ensure the reliability
367   and stability of our results, all numerical experiments were repeated 10 times, and the reported values
368
      represent the average of these repetitions. Details of all experimental specifications are provided in
      Appendix B.
369
370
      4.1     F EDERATED H YPER - REPRESENTATION L EARNING
371
372   In this learning task, the training and validation datasets are distributed among clients. The goal is
373   to learn a representation and a header on the joint training and validation datasets while preserving
374   privacy. Following Tarzanagh et al. (2022); Xiao et al. (2023), the problem can be formulated as a
375   special case of (1), given by
376                     n                                                      n
                   1X                                                       1X

              x    n i=1                                              y     n i=1

                   and Dval are the training and validation sets of client i ‚ąą [n], respectively. The function
380   fce is the cross-entropy loss, and rc is the regularization parameter used to ensure the (possible)
381   strong convexity condition. In previous experiments (Yang et al. (2023); Tarzanagh et al. (2022)), rc
382   was set to 0.05. We refer to Appendix B.1 for the details on the implementation and only discuss the
383   key model hyperparameter rc in the main text.

401   Robustness to rc. We test the sensitivity of MeFBO and other FBO algorithms to the model
402   hyperparameter rc. From Figure 1, we observe the following:
403
               ‚ÄĘ MeFBO is the most robust to the choice of the regularization parameter rc.
404
405            ‚ÄĘ Although the theoretical analyses of SimFBO, LFedNest, and FedNest rely on strong
406
                 convexity, they achieve the best accuracy when rc = 0, not at the default setting of
                 rc = 0.05. This indicates that the regularization technique used to enforce strong convexity
407
                 may degrade performance, highlighting the urgent need to design and study FBO algorithms
408
                 for non-convex scenarios.
409
               ‚ÄĘ In repeated experiments with 300 communication rounds, SimFBO (Yang et al. (2023))
410
                 achieves the best accuracy when the rc value is not equal to 0.07. For a more intuitive
411
                 comparison, please refer to Figure 5.
412
413   More comprehensive experimental results are presented in Figures 4 and 5 in appendix A.1.
414
415   4.2   F EDERATED L OSS F UNCTION T UNING ON I MBALANCED DATASET
416
417   Following the federated setting in Tarzanagh et al. (2022), the goal of this task is to tune a loss
418   function for learning on an imbalanced MNIST dataset distributed among clients. The specific
419
      formulation and experimental details are provided in Appendix B.2.
420   Results. Figure 2 illustrates the comparative performance of FBO algorithms in terms of test accuracy
421   versus communication rounds, employing local round ŌĄ = 3 for the i.i.d. setting and local round
422   ŌĄ = 1 for the non-i.i.d. setting. This experimental design serves to showcase the performance of
423   different algorithms with different local rounds under varying degrees of data heterogeneity.
424
               ‚ÄĘ It clearly shows that MeFBO achieves a faster convergence rate and higher accuracy under
425              different local round ŌĄ , both in the i.i.d and non-i.i.d. setting.
426
               ‚ÄĘ As illustrated in Figure 2 (b), in a highly heterogeneous environment, it is evident that
427
                 MeFBO demonstrates enhanced robustness as the number of communication rounds in-
428              creases.
429
430   More comprehensive experimental results are presented in Figures 6 and 7 in Appendix A.2. We also
431   test the sensitivity of MeFBO and the baselines to the regularization parameter rc. The results are
      summarized in Figures 8 and 9 in Appendix A.2, indicating that MeFBO is robust to the choice of rc.

483
      in Li et al. (2023), are provided in Appendix B.3.

523
524   5      C ONCLUSION
525
526   This paper investigates federated bilevel optimization problems with non-convex lower-level objec-
527   tives and introduces MeFBO, a novel, flexible, fully gradient-based algorithm. We provide a rigorous
528   convergence analysis and complexity analysis for our method with both fixed and decreasing step
529   sizes. Our results demonstrate that MeFBO achieves linear speedup with respect to the number of
530   clients in federated bilevel optimization, even in the absence of convexity in the lower-level objectives.
531   Experiments highlight the advantages of our proposed algorithms, particularly in scenarios involving
532
      non-convex lower-level objectives.

951
952   B       D ETAILS OF EXPERIMENTS
953
954   In this section, we present the specific configurations used in the experiments outlined in Section
955   4. For the federated bilevel learning experiments, we designate the number of workers as n =
956   100, and each local network is structured as a 2 or 3-layer multilayer perceptron with a hidden
957   dimension of 200 on a MNIST dataset. The hyperparameters are determined through a grid search,
958   taking into account both the convergence speed and algorithm stability, and we provide a detailed
959   report of these settings. For the baseline methods FedNest and LFedNest, we use their published
960
      codes in https://github.com/ucr-optml/FedNest. For SimFBO in federated hyper-
      representation, we use the source codes sent from the authors. The experiments were performed
961
      utilizing Python 3.7 on a computer equipped with an Intel(R) Xeon(R) Gold 5218R CPU @ 2.10GHz
962
      and an NVIDIA A100 GPU boasting 40GB of memory.
963
964
      B.1         F EDERATED HYPER - REPRESENTATION
965
966   In this section, we apply the MeFBO algorithm in Algorithm 1 to the task of federated hyper-
967   representation learning with a 2-layer MLP on MNIST Dataset with i.i.d. distribution and non-
968   i.i.d. distribution. The classic machine learning approach jointly learns a data representation and
969   downstream header on the training dataset. In contrast, bilevel representation learning Tarzanagh
970   et al. (2022) seeks to learn the data representation on the validation set while learning the header
971   on the training set. This bilevel representation learning procedure can be formulated as a bilevel
      optimization problem. In a federated representation learning scenario involving n = 100 clients, the

       B.3         F EDERATED DATA HYPER - CLEANING
1182
1183   In this section, we apply the MeFBO algorithm (Algorithm 1) to the data hyper-cleaning task using
1184   a 2-layer MLP on the MNIST dataset with both i.i.d. and non-i.i.d. distributions. Following the
1185   approach in Tarzanagh et al. (2022), we partition the MNIST dataset into training, validation, and test
1186   sets using both i.i.d. and non-i.i.d. methods. Inspired by the work of Li et al. (2023), to mitigate issues
1187   of data quality and heterogeneity in federated learning settings, a promising approach is federated
       data hyper-cleaning. This technique can be formulated as a federated bilevel optimization (FBO)

1216
1217   problem, where the upper-level objective aims to learn a globally optimal data cleaning policy, while
1218   the lower-level objectives correspond to the individual client objectives after applying the learned
1219   cleaning policy to their local datasets. Notably, the lower-level functions in this formulation exhibit
1220
       non-convexity, rendering the overall problem setting more challenging than the strongly convex case.
       In this experiment, we are presented with a noisy training dataset whose labels are corrupted by noise
1221
       with a corruption rate cr, along with a clean validation set. Our objective is to determine optimal
1222
       weights for the training samples such that a model learned over the weighted training set exhibits
1223   superior performance on the validation set. The problem can be formulated as :
1224

1334    The proof of Theorem C.1 is presented in Theorem D.11 in Appendix D.4. And there are several
1335    remarks about Theorem C.1.
1336    Decreasing step sizes. In contrast to fixed step sizes, the selection of decreasing step sizes can be
1337    independent of T. Moreover, the decay rate q of these step sizes influences the convergence rate: a
1338    larger q results in slower convergence.
1339
        Complexity analysis. The introduction of q increases the difficulty of analyzing sample complexities
1340    or communication complexities. Yet, an appropriate trade-off in its selection can optimize both
1341    convergence rate and these complexities.

1426   D.2    A UNIFIED PROOF SKETCH OF T HEOREMS 3.4 AND C.1
1427
1428   In Section 3, Theorems 3.4 and C.1 confirm that the MeFBO algorithm is not only straightforward to
1429
       implement but also guaranteed to converge theoretically. This section provides a detailed convergence
       analysis of the proposed federated bilevel optimization algorithm. The analysis involves two main
1430
       steps: first, deriving an upper bound for the residual function, and second, obtaining precise bounds
1431
       for the client drift terms. By employing Lyapunov function analysis and selecting step sizes carefully,
1432   we establish rigorous theoretical guarantees for the convergence behavior of Algorithm 1.
1433
1434
1435
       Step 1. Upper bound of residual function Rt (x(t) , y (t) ) with step size dependencies.
       By leveraging Assumptions 3.1 (ii), (iii), and 3.2, along with the L-smoothness properties of ő¶ct

                                                                                    (t) (t)
1438   where cőĽ is a positive constant, we derive the following inequality for Rt (x , y ):

1530   Step d. Controlling the client drifts.

1535          ‚ÄĘ Unlike in strongly convex settings, where certain iterates are bounded (e.g., Lemmas 1 and 2
1536            in Yang et al. (2023)), the unbounded nature of iterates in our setting complicates the control
1537            of client drifts, thereby introducing additional variability.

1550
       The proof is provided in the appendix, specifically in the proof of Lemma D.7. The client drifts are
1551
       bounded by the variances of the stochastic gradient estimators, the data heterogeneity measure ‚ąÜ2 as
1552
       defined in Assumption 3.2(iii), the client drifts themselves, and the distances of global iterates őł(t) to
1553
       their optimal solutions at each iteration t.
1554   All these terms can be controlled by appropriately adjusting the local step sizes ő∑x , ő∑y , and ő∑őł .
1555   By carefully tuning these step sizes, the impact of client drifts on the convergence analysis can be
1556   mitigated, ensuring improved stability and convergence.
1557
       Step e. Deriving inequality (33) through step size adjustment.
1558
       By combining steps a, b, c, and d, we ensure that the conditions in Lemma D.8 are satisfied through
1559
       appropriate adjustment of the step sizes. This guarantees that the coefficients of E‚ą•x(t+1) ‚ąí x(t) ‚ą•2 ,
1560
       E‚ą•őł(t) ‚ąí őłő≥‚ąó (x(t) , y (t) )‚ą•2 , and E‚ą•őł(t) ‚ąí őłő≥‚ąó (x(t) , y (t) )‚ą•2 are strictly negative. Consequently, the
1561
       inequality (33) is achieved.

       The following remarks provide crucial insights into the step size condition and the boundedness of
2585
       certain terms in our analysis:
2586
       Remark D.9. It is important to note that the condition for the step size in Lemma D.8 is indeed
2587
       satisfiable. We maintain the same inequality direction with the step size on the left-hand side and a
2588
       constant on the right-hand side. Furthermore, we ensure that the right-hand side is always positive,
2589   thereby guaranteeing the existence of a valid step size.
2590
2591   Building upon the step size condition, we now turn our attention to the boundedness of a key term in
       our analysis:

2754
       D.5    C OMPLEXITY ANALYSIS
2755
2756   Corollary D.12. Under the setting of Theorem 3.4, we have the following results:
2757
2758    (i) In the case of full client participation, setting ŌĄ = O(T ), the per-client sample complexity is
2759        ŌĄ T = O(ŌĶ‚ąí2 ) and the communication complexity T = O(ŌĶ‚ąí1 ).
2760
2761
        (ii) For partial client participation, setting ŌĄ = O(1), the per-client sample complexity is ŌĄ T =
             O(P ‚ąí2 ŌĶ‚ąí2 ) and the communication complexity T = O(P ‚ąí2 ŌĶ‚ąí2 ).
2762

       E     R ELATED WORK
2787
2788
       E.1   B ILEVEL OPTIMIZATION WITHOUT LLSC
2789
       Since the seminal work by Bracken & McGill (1973), numerous studies have proposed various
2790
       methods for addressing bilevel optimization problems. Extensive overviews of these approaches can
2791   be found in surveys Sinha et al. (2017); Liu et al. (2021a); Zhang et al. (2023). In this section, we
2792   provide a brief overview of relevant work on bilevel optimization (BLO) without the lower-level
2793   (LL) strong convexity assumption. Beyond the LL strong convexity assumption, Liu et al. (2021b)
2794   developed a method with initialization auxiliary and pessimistic trajectory truncation. Huang (2023a)
2795   proposed a momentum-based implicit gradient BLO algorithm and established a convergence analysis
2796   framework under a nondegenerate condition on the LL Hessian. Arbel & Mairal (2022) extended
2797   implicit differentiation to a class of non-convex LL functions with possibly degenerate critical
2798   points and developed unrolled optimization algorithms. Xiao et al. (2023) developed a generalized
2799
       alternating method for BLO with a non-convex LL objective. However, these works require second-
       order gradient information. In contrast, the value function reformulation of BLO was first utilized
2800
       in Liu et al. (2023) to develop BLO algorithms in machine learning using an interior-point method.
2801
       Subsequently, Liu et al. (2022) introduced a fully first-order value function-based BLO algorithm
2802   and established non-asymptotic convergence results. Recently, Shen & Chen (2023) proposed a
2803   penalty-based fully first-order BLO algorithm, relaxing the relatively restrictive assumption on the
2804   boundedness of both the upper-level (UL) and LL objectives present in Liu et al. (2022). Notably,
2805   these works involve a double-loop structure, which makes them challenging to employ in Federated
2806   Bilevel Optimization. To mitigate the requirement of single-loop structure, Liu et al. (2024); Yao
2807   et al. (2023) developed single-loop and Hessian-free gradient-based methods utilizing a Moreau
       envelope-based reformulation of bilevel optimization.


                                                         52
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


2808
       E.2   F EDERATED ( BILEVEL ) LEARNING
2809
2810   Federated Learning (FL) was initially proposed by Google to coordinate the collaborative training
2811   of a common task across thousands of clients while addressing data isolation and privacy con-
2812   cerns(McMahan et al., 2017). As the pioneering algorithm, FedAvg(McMahan et al., 2017) has
2813   effectively addressed the aforementioned issues; however, it has also given rise to a series of new
2814   challenges such as fairness, communication overhead, malicious participants and privacy (Mohri
2815   et al., 2019; Stich, 2019; Yu et al., 2019; Wang & Joshi, 2021; Bagdasaryan et al., 2020; Bhagoji
2816
       et al., 2019; Kim & Hong, 2019; A√Įvodji et al., 2019). To address these challenges, some research
       efforts have introduced a nested optimization structure, known as Federated Bilevel learning, such as
2817
       (Xing et al., 2022; Li et al., 2022; Zeng et al., 2021; Li et al., 2021b; Hu et al., 2024a; Huang et al.,
2818
       2022; Tolpegin et al., 2020; Sun et al., 2021; Zhang et al., 2021; Cheng et al., 2024). In response
2819   to the demand for solving problems with such model requirements, there have been some effective
2820   attempts. As one of the earliest methods of federated bilevel optimization(Tarzanagh et al., 2022),
2821   FedNest is a federated alternating stochastic gradient method based on AID-based hypergradient
2822   estimation to address general federated nested problems, which needs the federated hypergradient
2823   estimation. Additionally, there are other FBO algorithms based on AID-based hypergradient estima-
2824   tion(Huang et al., 2023). Xiao & Ji (2023) introduced a federated Bilevel Optimization algorithm
2825   with hypergradient estimation based on ITD-based hypergradient estimation. Recent FBO methods,
2826   such as SimFBO (Yang et al., 2023) and FedBiOAcc (Li et al., 2023), draw inspiration from SOBA
2827
       (Dagr√©ou et al., 2022). These approaches transform a linear system problem into a quadratic one, im-
       proving computational efficiency within a single-loop algorithmic framework. Notably, FedBiOAcc
2828
       incorporates a momentum-based technique. While these algorithms have made significant progress,
2829
       they continue to rely on Hessian matrix computations and are constrained by the requirement for
2830   lower-level strong convexity (LLSC).
2831
2832   In addition to the aforementioned works, there is a growing body of research on bilevel optimization
2833
       in asynchronous settings, such as those by Jiao et al. (2022) and Li et al. (2024). Furthermore, FBO
       has demonstrated promising practical applications, particularly in the fine-tuning of large language
2834
       models (LLMs) within federated settings. For instance, Wu et al. (2024) investigates the use of
2835
       FBO for local fine-tuning of LLMs. As noted in Table 2 of Wu et al. (2024), these models can be
2836   optimized using either single-level or bilevel approaches. Notably, the bilevel optimization method,
2837   FedBiOT, proposed by Wu et al. (2024), exhibits significant advantages over single-level optimization,
2838   especially in scenarios involving hierarchical problem structures.
2839

2841
2842
2843
2844

2846

       F                     A DDITIONAL EXPERIMENT RESULTS
2863
2864
       Extending beyond Section 4.3, we conducted comprehensive experiments with both expanded datasets
2865   and more sophisticated neural architectures. We evaluated MeFBO (Algorithm 1), SimFBO (Yang
2866   et al., 2023), FedNest (Tarzanagh et al., 2022), and LFedNest (Tarzanagh et al., 2022) on additional
2867   data hyper-cleaning tasks. The experiments employed either a 2-layer MLP (as in Section 4.3) or a
2868   7-layer CNN (LeCun et al., 1998) on Fashion MNIST (Xiao et al., 2017) and CIFAR-10 (Krizhevsky
2869   et al., 2009) datasets. Following (Xiao & Ji, 2023), we also implemented a 2-layer MLP architecture
2870   for CIFAR-10.
2871   Fashion-MNIST represents a moderate increase in complexity compared to MNIST, featuring fashion
2872   items rather than handwritten digits. Both datasets contain 70,000 grayscale images (28√ó28). CIFAR-
2873   10, comprising 60,000 color images (32√ó32, RGB), presents greater complexity due to its real-world
2874   object representations and multi-channel color information.
2875   Figure 14 summarizes our experimental findings, yielding several key insights:
2876
       Results on Larger Datasets and Complex Neural Architectures. Figure 14 presents comparative
2877   performance analyses across different network architectures and datasets under i.i.d. settings with a
2878   corruption rate (cr) of 0.7:
2879
2880                              ‚ÄĘ Fashion MNIST with 2-layer MLP: As shown in Figure 14(a), while maintaining consis-
2881                                tency with Section 4.3‚Äôs architecture but scaling to a larger dataset, MeFBO demonstrated
2882                                superior convergence characteristics, achieving the highest test accuracy (approximately 85
2883                              ‚ÄĘ CIFAR-10 with 7-layer CNN: As illustrated in Figure 14(b), when tested on the more
2884                                complex CIFAR-10 dataset with a 7-layer CNN architecture, MeFBO maintained its supe-
2885                                rior performance, achieving the highest accuracy among all methods. Notably, SimFBO
2886                                encountered memory constraints that prevented its execution on this larger-scale task.
2887                              ‚ÄĘ Architecture Comparison on CIFAR-10: Following (Xiao & Ji, 2023), we compared the
2888                                performance of 2-layer MLP and 7-layer CNN architectures using MeFBO on CIFAR-10, as
2889                                shown in Figure 14(c). The MLP exhibited faster initial convergence but plateaued at a lower
2890                                accuracy, while the CNN achieved higher ultimate accuracy despite slower convergence.
2891                                However, these performance levels suggest room for improvement. We hypothesize that
2892
                                    architectural limitations may be constraining performance on this specific task, warranting
                                    further investigation in future research.
